--- SHEET: Overview  (dim=A1:H17, max_row=17, max_col=8) ---
NORDIC INTERNATIONAL SCHOOL — GRAMMAR CURRICULUM OVERVIEW (GRADES 1-11)
Target pathway: Grade 6 = KET (A2) | Grade 7 = PET (B1) | Grade 9 = FCE (B2) | Grade 11 = CAE (C1)
Prepared for: Head of School — Nordic International School, Lima, Peru
Grade | CEFR | Cambridge Exam | MINEDU Ciclo | # Grammar Topics | Key Grammar Focus | Suggested Weekly Hours | Teaching Approach
1st | Pre-A1 | — | Ciclo III | 12 | Verb to be, present tenses, basic modals, prepositions, Wh-questions | ~5 hrs/week | Play-based, TPR, songs, visual support, storytelling
2nd | Pre-A1→Starters | Starters | Ciclo III | 9 | Past simple expansion, future with will, pronouns, comparatives, connectors | ~5 hrs/week | Play-based, Starters exam format intro, picture-based tasks
3rd | A1 | — | Ciclo IV | 7 | Subject-verb agreement, sentence types, superlatives, modals, dictionary skills | ~5 hrs/week | Games, group work, guided writing, vocabulary journals
4th | A1→Movers | Movers | Ciclo IV | 10 | Tense contrast, reflexive/relative pronouns, tag questions, compound sentences | ~5 hrs/week | Movers prep tasks, role-play, treasure hunts, creative writing
5th | A2 | — | Ciclo V | 8 | Past progressive, time clauses, collocations, connectors, figurative language | ~6 hrs/week | Opinion writing, debates, poetry, proofreading workshops
6th | A2→KET | KET | Ciclo V | 8 | Present perfect, full modals, adverb placement, adjective order, phrasal verbs | ~7 hrs/week | KET exam prep, letter writing, surveys, cultural comparisons
7th | B1→PET | PET | Ciclo VI | 11 | Past perfect, conditionals 0-1, expanded modals, connectors, register awareness | ~7 hrs/week | PET exam prep, news writing, debates, for-and-against essays
8th | B1+ | PET consol. | Ciclo VI | 8 | Word formation, future continuous, conditionals 0-1-2, passive, reported speech | ~7 hrs/week | Interview simulations, research reports, journalism, process writing
9th | B2→FCE | FCE | Ciclo VII | 7 | Conditional type 3, passive advanced, complex modals, word transformation | ~8 hrs/week | FCE exam prep, creative fiction, academic debates, case studies
10th | B2+ | FCE consol. | Ciclo VII | 8 | Mixed conditionals, subjunctive, complex relatives, advanced transformations | ~8 hrs/week | CAE bridge, ethical discussions, research papers, formal proposals
11th | C1→CAE | CAE | Ciclo VII | 10 | Inversion, cleft sentences, discourse markers, advanced verb patterns, impersonal passive | ~8 hrs/week | CAE exam prep, literary criticism, academic seminars, professional writing
TOTAL |  |  |  | 98

--- SHEET: Grade 1st  (dim=A1:G21, max_row=21, max_col=7) ---
NORDIC INTERNATIONAL SCHOOL — GRAMMAR CURRICULUM: GRADE 1ST
CEFR Level: | Pre-A1
Cambridge Exam: | —
Recommended Hours: | ~5 hrs/week
MINEDU Alignment: | Ciclo III (inicio) – Textos orales breves
Grammar Topic | Key Structures / Forms | Suggested Teaching Themes & Contexts | Example Sentences for Class | Classroom Activities & Tasks | Cambridge Exam Link
Verb "to be" (all forms) | Affirmative, negative, interrogative | My family & me
All about me poster
Classroom introductions | "I am 7 years old." "She is my sister." "Are you happy?" | Introduce self and family
Describe people
Ask simple questions | KET foundations: basic sentence structure
Present continuous | Subject + am/is/are + verb-ing | What are we doing now?
Classroom actions
Mime games | "I am drawing." "She is reading a book." | Describe ongoing actions
Play action games
Describe pictures | Listening for actions in context
Simple present | Habits, routines, facts; 3rd person -s | Daily routines
My school day
Animals and their habits | "I go to school at 8." "She eats breakfast." "Dogs bark." | Talk about daily life
Describe animal behaviors
Interview classmates about routines | Builds toward KET present tense tasks
Past simple (common verbs) | Regular (-ed) and irregular (went, saw, ate) | My weekend
Story retelling
Yesterday's adventures | "I played in the park." "We went to grandma's house." | Tell simple past stories
Describe weekend activities
Sequence past events | Foundation for narrative writing
Introduction of "going to" | Subject + am/is/are + going to + verb | My plans
What are you going to do?
Weekend planning | "I am going to visit my friend." "We are going to play." | Talk about future plans
Make simple predictions
Plan a class event | Future expression foundations
Modals: can / can't | Ability and inability | Animals & abilities
Superhero powers
Talent show | "A fish can swim." "I can't fly." "Can you ride a bike?" | Describe abilities
Compare what animals can do
Ask about classmates' abilities | KET ability expressions
Introduction of "would" | Polite requests, preferences | At the restaurant
Wishes & preferences
Polite requests | "I would like a pet." "Would you like some water?" | Order food politely
Express preferences
Role-play service situations | Polite language for KET speaking
Demonstratives: this / that | Near vs. far reference | Classroom objects
Show and tell
Shopping role-play | "This is my pencil." "That is your bag." | Identify objects near and far
Present objects to the class
Point and describe | Referencing in context
Prepositions of place | on, in, under, next to, between, behind, in front of | Where is it?
Hide and seek
My bedroom description | "The cat is under the table." "The book is on the shelf." | Describe room layouts
Give/follow location instructions
Play positional games | Spatial descriptions for KET
Wh-questions | Who, what, where, when, why, how, which | Getting to know you
20 questions game
Interview a friend | "Where do you live?" "What is your name?" "How old are you?" | Ask personal questions
Conduct mini-interviews
Answer about self | Core question forms for all Cambridge exams
Nouns: proper & common, singular & plural | Capitalization rules, regular/irregular plurals | My world categories
Noun sorting games
City vs. country | "Lima is a city." "Dogs are animals." "Children play." | Categorize nouns
Practice plural forms
Capitalize properly | Spelling and writing conventions
Adjectives (describing words) | Colors, sizes, feelings, qualities | Describe everything
Art descriptions
Feelings journal | "The big red ball." "A happy girl." "A scary movie." | Describe objects, people, feelings
Create descriptive art
Expand vocabulary | Descriptive language for KET writing
Total grammar topics: 12

Grammar Topic | Key Structures / Forms | Suggested Teaching Themes & Contexts | Example Sentences for Class | Classroom Activities & Tasks | Cambridge Exam Link
Present simple & progressive (3rd person) | Contrast: habits vs. now; he/she -s rule | People around me
Celebrity daily lives
What's happening now? | "She plays piano." "He is eating lunch right now." | Compare routines vs. current actions
Describe what people do vs. are doing
Starters listening practice | Key distinction for all Cambridge exams
Past simple (regular & irregular expanded) | More irregular verbs; negative and question forms | Yesterday's adventure
Famous events
Story chains | "We visited the museum." "I saw a dinosaur." "Did you go?" | Tell detailed past stories
Ask about past experiences
Write simple past narratives | Past tense mastery for Starters
Future with "will" | Predictions, promises, spontaneous decisions | Predictions game
Promises to friends
Weather forecasts | "It will rain tomorrow." "I will help you." "I think she'll win." | Make predictions
Promise actions
Discuss weather | Future tense variety
Personal & possessive pronouns | I/me, my/mine, he/him, his, etc. | Whose is it?
Lost and found
Family tree descriptions | "This is her book." "That pen is mine." "They are his shoes." | Practice ownership
Describe family relationships
Play guessing games | Pronoun accuracy for Starters
Demonstratives: these / those | Near/far plural forms | Shopping and choosing
Classroom sorting
Market role-play | "These apples are red." "Those bananas are yellow." | Compare groups of objects
Shop at a pretend market
Sort and categorize | Expanding demonstrative range
There is / There are | Existence, describing places | My neighborhood
Dream house
Picture descriptions | "There is a park." "There are two shops." "Is there a pool?" | Describe places
Design dream rooms
Answer Starters picture tasks | Starters Part 1 picture descriptions
Object pronouns | me, you, him, her, it, us, them | Giving and receiving
Classroom instructions
Story retelling | "Give it to me." "I saw them at school." "Tell her the answer." | Follow and give instructions
Retell stories with correct pronouns
Practice in dialogues | Accurate pronoun use
Linking words: and, but, then, because | Basic connectors for longer sentences | Telling longer stories
Reason giving
Sequencing events | "I was hungry but there was no food." "I like cats because they are soft." | Connect ideas in writing
Give reasons
Write 3-sentence stories | Foundation for Starters writing
Comparative adjectives | -er / more + adj. + than | Comparing animals
World records
Family comparisons | "An elephant is bigger than a dog." "Math is more difficult than art." | Compare two things
Make factual comparisons
Play comparison games | Comparison structures for later exams

Build correct sentences
Edit peers' work | Accuracy foundation for all writing

Life story booklet
Time travel stories | "Yesterday I went... Today I am... Tomorrow I will..." | Create personal timelines
Write mini-biographies
Tell time-travel adventures | Tense consolidation before Movers
Synonyms & antonyms | Words with similar/opposite meanings | Word power challenges
Vocabulary expansion games
Descriptive writing | "Happy = glad, joyful. Hot ≠ cold. Big ≠ small." | Build word webs
Play matching games
Use varied vocabulary in writing | Vocabulary range for Cambridge
Dictionary skills | Alphabetical order, pronunciation symbols, definitions | Word explorers
Dictionary races
New word journals | Look up meaning, spelling, pronunciation of new words | Find words independently
Keep vocabulary journals
Learn phonetic symbols | Independent learning skills

Play spelling games

--- SHEET: Grade 9th  (dim=A1:G16, max_row=16, max_col=7) ---
NORDIC INTERNATIONAL SCHOOL — GRAMMAR CURRICULUM: GRADE 9TH
CEFR Level: | B2 → FCE
Cambridge Exam: | Cambridge FCE (B2)
Recommended Hours: | ~8 hrs/week
MINEDU Alignment: | Ciclo VII (inicio) – Textos diversos, vocabulario variado, situaciones formales/informales
Grammar Topic | Key Structures / Forms | Suggested Teaching Themes & Contexts | Example Sentences for Class | Classroom Activities & Tasks | Cambridge Exam Link

▸ Bloom: Analyse | Modal verbs: should, ought to, might, could (advice/possibility)

▸ Bloom: Analyse–Evaluate | Past Simple + Past Continuous: while/when
Past Perfect: introduction (I had already…)

▸ Bloom: Evaluate–Create

 |  | Language Conventions | Present Simple (all persons): I go, she goes, we play

Should/shouldn't (advice – intro)
Conjunctions: and, but, because
▸ Bloom: Apply | There is / There are (singular & plural)
Some / any with plural nouns
Comparatives: bigger than, faster than (intro)
▸ Bloom: Apply | Verb 'feel' + adjective: I feel happy/sick

Simple question: Were you ill yesterday?
▸ Bloom: Understand–Apply | Present Simple: he/she + helping verbs

--- SHEET: English Scope 2026  (dim=A1:AB220, max_row=220, max_col=28) ---
BENCHMARK --------------> |  | By the end of the Nursery, students will be able to communicate orally using everyday vocabulary and gestures. They will understand the images or stories in the text. They will use spontaneous strokes to communicate. | By the end of  Pre-Kindergarten, students will be able to communicate orally using frequently used vocabulary and gestures, building on their prior knowledge. They will understand information from texts or illustrations. They will use strokes to communicate their ideas and emotions. | By the end of  Pre-Kindergarten, students will be able to communicate orally using frequently used vocabulary and gestures, building on their prior knowledge. They will understand information from texts or illustrations. They will use strokes to communicate their ideas and emotions. | By the end of Kindergarten, students will be able to communicate orally using a frequently used and extensive vocabulary to express their ideas and emotions. They will obtain and understand general ideas from written texts or illustrations. They will use strokes following directionality to record and communicate ideas and emotions. | By the end of Grade 1, the student can understand clear spoken English aided by pictures and gestures. They can describe themselves and their world using simple sentences and share opinions about stories they hear. Reading comprehension includes understanding key information and making basic connections in simple texts with pictures. Writing skills involve creating short stories and messages with pictures, using familiar words and organizing ideas clearly. |  | By the end of Grade 2, the student demonstrates understanding of slightly more complex spoken English, including drawing simple inferences. They can express themselves on various topics. Reading skills progress to grasping the main message and making deeper connections in more complex texts. Writing develops into creating longer and more detailed texts with proper spelling and connectors, incorporating self-reflection to ensure clarity | By the end of grade 3, students will be able to express themselves and comprehend basic English orally, using simple vocabulary and grammar to talk about personal experiences and preferences, while beginning to use nonverbal cues for emphasis. They will read simple structured texts, identifying key information and making straightforward connections, and write brief texts with basic connectors and grammar, focusing on clear expression of ideas. | By the end of grade 4, students will enhance their oral communication to interpret a speaker's intention and articulate opinions on texts, using everyday language more effectively. They will read more complex texts, making inferences and expressing personal viewpoints with increased independence. In writing, they will organize their ideas around central themes more effectively, employing a wider range of connectors for coherence, and reflect on their writing for improved clarity. | By the end of grade 5, students will be able to communicate orally in English, understanding explicit information and beginning to make inferences about the speaker’s intentions. They will express their ideas clearly, using appropriate pronunciation and basic connectors to organize thoughts around a topic, and will effectively use nonverbal cues to support their communication. In reading, they will navigate simple texts, distinguishing clear information and making initial inferences to understand overall meaning, while in writing, they will create short texts with a focus on audience and purpose, organizing ideas in structured paragraphs and using basic grammatical constructions for clarity. | By the end of grade 6, students will advance in oral communication, not only capturing explicit information but also inferring deeper meanings and interpreting speaker intentions with more sophistication. They will express complex ideas more fluently, employing a broader vocabulary and coordinated connectors, and refine their use of nonverbal communication. Their reading skills will include a thorough analysis of texts, distinguishing subtle differences and making inferences to construct meaning, and their writing will reflect an improved ability to adapt texts to the audience, develop ideas around central themes, and employ cohesive devices for well-organized paragraphs. | By the end of grade 7, students will be skilled in orally communicating in English, able to infer and interpret detailed information from conversations and texts, including the intentions behind spoken words. They will effectively use nonverbal and paraverbal cues to enhance their speech, organize ideas around central themes using relevant vocabulary and grammar, and actively participate in discussions about familiar and past events. Their reading will involve critically engaging with texts to integrate information, make inferences, and evaluate language use. In writing, they will produce medium-length texts that are clearly organized, using cohesive devices and a mix of everyday and complex grammar for clarity, reflecting on their work with insight. | By the end of grade 8, students will refine their oral communication to more deeply understand and convey complex information, using nuanced nonverbal cues for emphasis and engaging in sophisticated exchanges on a broad range of topics. They will critically read texts, integrating advanced elements and making inferences to construct comprehensive interpretations, and apply critical thinking to evaluate texts' language and intentions. In writing, they will adeptly tailor texts to specific audiences and purposes, organizing ideas into coherent, structured paragraphs with sophisticated vocabulary and grammar, ensuring clarity through precise orthography, and engaging in thoughtful reflection and evaluation of their written work.

Communicates orally in English 
as a foreign language | It is defined as a dynamic interaction between one or more interlocutors to communicate their ideas and emotions. This involves an effective understanding and production of language, as it assumes an active process of constructing the meaning of various types of texts that one expresses or listens to, in order to achieve one's purposes. In this process, the student employs skills, knowledge, and attitudes from oral language and the world around them.

This competency also involves the appropriate use of different conversational strategies, considering the modes of politeness according to the sociocultural context. This includes taking turns at the right time, enriching and contributing to the conversation topic, and maintaining the thematic thread in order to negotiate, persuade, cooperate, among other objectives.

To construct the meaning of oral texts, it is essential to view orality as a social activity in which the student interacts with different sociocultural groups or communities. By doing so, they have the opportunity to use oral language in a creative and responsible manner, taking into account the impact of what is said | Obtains information from oral texts | consists of retrieving and extracting explicit information

Reads different types of texts
in English as a 
foreign language | It is defined as a dynamic interaction between the reader, the text and the sociocultural contexts.
that frame the reading. It is a critical understanding because it involves an active process
construction of the meaning of the various types of texts that are read through processes of
literal and inferential understanding, interpretation and reflection. In such processes, the student
brings into play skills, knowledge and attitudes coming from their reading experience and
world around him, becoming aware of the possibilities and limitations offered by the
language, communication and meaning.
This competence also implies that the student is aware that reading texts
fulfills specific purposes, such as enjoying, solving a problem or question, following
instructions, investigate, defend a position, among others.
Likewise, the competition considers the reading of various types of texts, that is, these texts
They belong to different genres, periods and authors, and are located in spaces and times
determined. This textual diversity is presented in different formats and supports, such as
printed, digital and multimodal, each with its characteristics and particularities.
To construct the meaning of the texts they read, it is essential that the student participate in
social reading practices in English that occur in different groups or communities
sociocultural. By engaging in such reading practices, the student contributes his or her
integral development, as well as that of their own community, in addition to knowing and interacting with
sociocultural contexts other than your own | Obtains information from written text

Writes different types of text
in English as a foreign language | This competence is defined as the use of written language to construct meanings in the
text and communicate them to others. It is a reflective process because it involves the adaptation and
organization of the texts considering the contexts and the communicative purpose, as well as
the permanent review of what is written with the aim of improving it.
The student brings into play different types of knowledge and resources coming from his or her experience.
with written language and the world around him. Uses the alphabetical system and a set
of writing conventions, as well as different strategies to expand ideas, emphasize or
nuance meanings in the texts she writes. With this, he becomes aware of the possibilities and
limitations offered by language, communication and meaning. This is crucial in a time
dominated by new technologies that have transformed the nature of written communication.
To construct the meaning of the texts you write, it is essential to assume writing as
a social practice that allows participation in different groups or sociocultural communities.
In addition to participating in social life, this competition involves other purposes, such as
construction of knowledge or the aesthetic use of language. By engaging with writing,
The possibility of interacting with other people using the written language of
creative and responsible manner, taking into account its impact on others.

Unit 1  |  |  | Reading for Literature:Ask and answer questions about key details in a text( with prompting and support)

Guided Questioning: Teach students how to ask questions before, during, and after reading to deepen comprehension.
Textual Evidence: Emphasize the importance of finding evidence in the text to support answers to questions.
Promoting Discussion: Encourage group discussions to enhance comprehension and critical thinking.


Reading for Informational Text: Ask and answer questions about key detials in a text ( witht prompting and support)

Text Structure Awareness: Teach students to recognize the structure of informational texts, such as headings, subheadings, and captions.
Identifying Key Details: Help students identify and focus on key details that answer questions about the text.
Graphic Organizers: Introduce graphic organizers like charts or diagrams to visually represent information.


Reading Skills: Demonstrates understanding of the organization and basic features of print

Print Concepts: Teach students about print concepts such as left-to-right progression, top-to-bottom orientation, and the concept of a word.
Text Features: Introduce common text features like titles, headings, and punctuation marks.
Tracking Text: Encourage students to use their finger to track text as they read, fostering one-to-one correspondence.

Reading Skills: know and apply grade-level phonics and word analysis skills in decoding words

Textual Evidence Awareness:
Explicit Reference Teaching: Emphasize the importance of explicitly referring to the text for answers. Teach students to provide evidence from the text to support their responses.

Question Formation:
Analyzing Question Types: Instruct students on different question types (e.g., who, what, where, when, why, how) and guide them in crafting responses that directly address the specific question asked.

Socratic Seminars:
Facilitating Discussions: Implement Socratic seminars or class discussions where students practice asking and answering questions about a text. Emphasize the use of textual evidence during these exchanges.

Annotation Skills:

Teaching Annotation Techniques: Teach students how to annotate texts effectively. Encourage them to underline, highlight, or make notes directly in the text to aid in answering questions later.
Critical Thinking Tasks:

Higher-order Questioning: Gradually introduce higher-order thinking questions that require deeper analysis. Prompt students to provide thoughtful responses backed by evidence from the text.

understand that words are seperated by spaces in print


recognize and name all uper and lowercase letters of the alphabet

Phonemic Awareness: Develop phonemic awareness by helping students connect spoken words to written sequences of letters.
Print Awareness: Emphasize the concept of spaces between words and the understanding that written language is a representation of spoken language.
Letter Recognition: Teach students to recognize and name all uppercase and lowercase letters of the alphabet.
Multisensory Learning: Incorporate a variety of sensory modalities into activities to enhance letter recognition and print awareness.

Metacognitive Reflection:

Unit 1  |  |  | Writing: Use a combination of drawing, dictating, and writing to compose opinion pieces in which they tell a reader the topic or the name of the book they are writing about and state an opinion or preference about the topic or book


Building Writing Readiness:Drawing Component: Recognize the importance of drawing as a precursor to writing. Encourage students to express their thoughts through drawings before transitioning to writing.

Topic and Opinion Development:Clear Topic Identification: Guide students in clearly identifying the topic of their opinion piece. Ensure they can articulate the subject or name of the book they are writing about.

Expressing Opinions: Emphasize the development of a clear opinion or preference. Teach students to state their viewpoint in a straightforward manner.
Multimodal Composition:

Combining Modalities: Help students understand the value of using multiple modalities—drawing, dictating, and writing—in the writing process. This allows for a more inclusive and expressive approach to composition.

Guided Dictation:Supportive Dictation: For early writers, provide opportunities for dictation. Allow students to express their thoughts verbally while you transcribe their words. This helps bridge the gap between spoken language and written expression.


Individual Expression:Encourage Personal Expression: Foster a supportive environment where students feel comfortable expressing their unique opinions and preferences. Celebrate individual perspectives in their writing.


Modeling and Shared Writing:Model the Process: Demonstrate the process of composing an opinion piece by combining drawing, dictation, and writing. Show how to integrate these modalities cohesively.

Shared Writing Activities: Engage in shared writing activities where the class collaboratively composes opinion pieces. This allows students to observe the writing process in action. | Writing: write opinion pieces in which they introduce the topic or name the book they are writing about , states an opinion, supply a reason for the opinion and provide some sense of closure  | Writing: Write opinion pieces in which they introduce the topic or book they are writing about state an opinoin, supply reasons that support the opinion, use linking words to connect opinion and reasons, provide a concluding statement | Write opinion pieces on topics or texts, supporting a point of view with  reasons, organizational structure that lists reaons, introduces the topic or text they are writing about, state an opinion, and create an organizatioanl structure that listas reasons, provides reasons that support opinion using linking words and phrases to connect opinion and reaons, provides a concluding statement  | Write opinion pieces on topics or text , supporting a point of view with reasons and information,

•  Retelling the story

Focus: Building basic vocabulary and sentence structure

Orthographic Conventions: Capitalization at the beginning of sentences.
